import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a 2D transposed convolution operation with asymmetric input, asymmetric kernel, 
    grouped, padded, and dilated.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (tuple): Size of the convolution kernel (height, width).
        stride (tuple, optional): Stride of the convolution (height, width). Defaults to (1, 1).
        padding (tuple, optional): Padding applied to the input (height, width). Defaults to (0, 0).
        dilation (tuple, optional): Spacing between kernel elements (height, width). Defaults to (1, 1).
        groups (int, optional): Number of blocked connections from input channels to output channels. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: tuple, stride: tuple = (1, 1), padding: tuple = (0, 0), dilation: tuple = (1, 1), groups: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose2d = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=bias)
        
    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 2D transposed convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose2d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = (3, 5)
height = 128
width = 256
stride = (2, 3)
padding = (1, 2)
dilation = (2, 1)
groups = 4

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation, groups]

Specifically: - Insert (stride[0]-1) zeros between rows and (stride[1]-1) zeros between columns - Pad the result with appropriate padding - Then do a regular convolution Actually, the standard equivalence: ConvTranspose2d(x, weight, stride, padding) = Conv2d(upsample(x, stride), weight, padding=dilation*(kernel_size-1)-padding) Wait, let me be more careful. The effective kernel size is dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + 1. A general approach: For ConvTranspose2d with parameters (stride, padding, dilation, groups): 1. Padding in Conv2d corresponds to removing border elements in ConvTranspose2d, which is achieved by changing the padding in the equivalent operation. I think it's safer to just implement ConvTranspose2d directly using the "scatter and accumulate" approach, or use the upsample+conv2d equivalence. Let me verify the upsample+conv2d approach more carefully with PyTorch: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn in_ch, out_ch, k, s, p, d, g = 32, 64, (3, 5), (2, 3), (1, 2), (2, 1), 4 x = torch.randn(2, in_ch, 128, 256) conv_t = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_ch, out_ch, k, stride=s, padding=p, dilation=d, groups=g, bias=False) out = conv_t(x) # Now try the equivalent N, C_in, H_in, W_in = x.shape H_out = (H_in - 1) * s[0] - 2 * p[0] + d[0] * (k[0] - 1) + 1 W_out = (W_in - 1) * s[1] - 2 * p[1] + d[1] * (k[1] - 1) + 1 # Upsample by inserting zeros x_up = torch.zeros(N, C_in, H_in * s[0], W_in * s[1]) x_up[:, :, ::s[0], ::s[1]] = x # Pad pad_h = d[0] * (k[0] - 1) - p[0] pad_w = d[1] * (k[1] - 1) - p[1] x_up = torch.nn.functional.pad(x_up, (pad_w, pad_w, pad_h, pad_h)) # Conv2d conv = nn.Conv2d(in_ch, out_ch, k, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=d, groups=g, bias=False) with torch.no_grad(): conv.weight.copy_(conv_t.weight) out2 = conv(x_up) print(out.shape, out2.shape) print((out - out2).abs().max()) ``` Actually, I'm not 100% sure about this equivalence. Let me think about it differently. The simplest and most correct approach is to implement ConvTranspose2d as: For each output position (b, c_out, h_out, w_out): output[b, c_out, h_out, w_out] = sum over c_in, kh, kw of input[b, c_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] where h_in and w_in are computed from h_out, w_out, kh, kw. For ConvTranspose2d, the relationship between input and output coordinates is: Given output coordinate h_out, input coordinate h_in, and kernel position kh: h_out = h_in * stride[0] + kh * dilation[0] - padding[0] * 2 + ... wait Actually for ConvTranspose2d, the standard formula is: For each input position (h_in, w_in), we place a scaled copy of the kernel at output position: h_out_start = h_in * stride[0] - padding[0] w_out_start = w_in * stride[1] - padding[1] And then for each kernel position (kh, kw): h_out = h_out_start + kh * dilation[0] w_out = w_out_start + kw * dilation[1] if h_out and w_out are within bounds, we add input[:, c_in, h_in, w_in] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] to output[:, c_out, h_out, w_out]. Wait, but actually the PyTorch ConvTranspose2d handles padding differently. Let me think about this more carefully. For PyTorch ConvTranspose2d: The output size is given by: H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride[0] - 2 * padding[0] + dilation[0] * (kernel_size[0] - 1) + output_padding[0] + 1 And the actual computation: each input element is multiplied by the weight and scattered to the output with stride spacing, starting from position -padding and with dilation. Let me see... In the simplest case, if stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1: output[h, w] += sum_{kh, kw} input[h - kh, w - kw] * weight[kh, kw] Wait, no. For ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, padding=0: output = conv_transpose(input, weight) is equivalent to cross-correlation of input with weight (not convolution), because convtranspose is the adjoint of conv. Actually, for ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1: output[b, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw} input[b, c_in, h - kh, w - kw] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] This is because ConvTranspose2d with stride=1, padding=0 is equivalent to Conv2d with stride=1, padding=kernel_size-1 using the same weight (up to a flip?). Wait, let me be more precise. In PyTorch, Conv2d computes: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, stride*h + kh, stride*w + kw] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] (with appropriate padding, dilation, etc. - this is an oversimplification) Actually, PyTorch Conv2d (with groups=1, stride=1, dilation=1) computes cross-correlation: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, h + kh - pad_h, w + kw - pad_w] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] And ConvTranspose2d is the adjoint operation. So if Conv2d: I -> O, then ConvTranspose2d: O -> I satisfies <Conv2d(I), O> = <I, ConvTranspose2d(O)>, where < , > is the Frobenius inner product. For Conv2d with stride=1, padding=p: output[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw} input[n, c_in, h + kh - p, w + kw - p] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] The transpose would map O -> I: input[n, c_in, h', w'] = sum_{c_out, kh, kw} output[n, c_out, h' - kh + p, w' - kw + p] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] Or equivalently: For each output position of the transpose (which is the input of the transpose), we have: transpose_input[n, c_out, h, w] contributes to transpose_output[n, c_in, h - kh + p, w - kw + p] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] So for the output of transpose at position (h', w'): transpose_output[n, c_in, h', w'] = sum_{c_out, kh, kw} transpose_input[n, c_out, h' + kh - p, w' + kw - p] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] Wait, that would mean for stride=1, padding=p: ConvTranspose2d(input, weight, padding=p) = Conv2d(input, weight, padding=k-1-p) Let me verify with a simple example: ```python import torch x = torch.randn(1, 1, 4, 4) w = torch.randn(1, 1, 3, 3) # ConvTranspose2d with padding=1 ct = torch.nn.ConvTranspose2d(1, 1, 3, padding=1, bias=False) ct.weight.data = w out1 = ct(x) # Conv2d with padding=3-1-1=1 c = torch.nn.Conv2d(1, 1, 3, padding=1, bias=False) c.weight.data = w out2 = c(x) print((out1 - out2).abs().max()) ``` Actually, I think the equivalence is not exact because Conv2d and ConvTranspose2d may flip the kernel differently. Let me think... In PyTorch, Conv2d computes cross-correlation (no flip), and ConvTranspose2d uses the same weight orientation as Conv2d (it is the transpose of Conv2d, not the inverse). For Conv2d: O = Conv2d(I, W) where O[h, w] = sum_{kh, kw} I[h + kh - pad, w + kw - pad] * W[kh, kw] For ConvTranspose2d: If O = Conv2d(I, W), then I = ConvTranspose2d(O, W). So if O[h, w] = sum_{kh, kw} I[h + kh - p, w + kw - p] * W[kh, kw] Then for any output index (h', w') of I: I[h', w'] = sum_{h, w, kh, kw} O[h, w] * W[kh, kw] * delta(h' = h + kh - p, w' = w + kw - p) = sum_{h, w, kh, kw} O[h, w] * W[kh, kw] * delta(h = h' - kh + p, w = w' - kw + p) So I[h', w'] = sum_{kh, kw} O[h' - kh + p, w' - kw + p] * W[kh, kw] This is exactly a cross-correlation of O with W, with padding = k - 1 - p. Wait, but with stride. Let's generalize to stride. Conv2d with stride=s: O[h, w] = sum_{kh, kw} I[s*h + kh - p, s*w + kw - p] * W[kh, kw] For ConvTranspose2d, we want I[s*h + kh - p, s*w + kw - p] = sum over h, w, kh, kw of O[h, w] * W[kh, kw] such that the indices match. So for any position (h', w') in I: I[h', w'] = sum_{h, w, kh, kw} O[h, w] * W[kh, kw] * delta(h' = s*h + kh - p, w' = s*w + kw - p) For a given (h', w'), when does h' = s*h + kh - p for some integers h, kh with 0 <= kh < k? h = (h' - kh + p) / s This requires (h' - kh + p) to be divisible by s and h to be in valid range. So I[h', w'] = sum_{kh, kw} O[(h' - kh + p) / s, (w' - kw + p) / s] * W[kh, kw] where the sum is over kh, kw such that the indices are valid and divisible. This can be rewritten as: For each O[h, w], it contributes to I at positions: h' = s*h + kh - p w' = s*w + kw - p for all valid kh, kw. So the algorithm is: For each input element O[h, w], scale it by W[kh, kw] and add to I[s*h + kh - p, s*w + kw - p]. Now for dilation d, the formula becomes: h' = s*h + d*kh - p w' = s*w + d*kw - p Now let's verify with the upsample+conv2d approach: If we upsample O by inserting s-1 zeros between elements, so that O_upsample[s*h, s*w] = O[h, w], then: I[h', w'] = sum_{kh, kw} O_upsample[h' + p - d*kh, w' + p - d*kw] * W[kh, kw] This is equivalent to: Conv2d(O_upsample_padded, W, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=d) where O_upsample_padded = pad(O_upsample, (p - d*(k-1), p - d*(k-1))) ??? Let me think about the im2col/col2im approach. For a regular conv2d: - We extract patches from the input using im2col, resulting in a matrix of shape (batch, out_H * out_W, in_C * kH * kW) - Multiply by weight matrix of shape (out_C, in_C * kH * kW) -> gives (batch, out_H * out_W, out_C) - Reshape For convtranspose2d, the "transpose" means: - We can reshape the input to (batch, in_H * in_W, in_C) - The weight is of shape (out_C, in_C, kH, kW) or for groups (out_C/g, in_C/g, kH, kW) - For each group, we do something like input reshaped -> (in_H * in_W, in_C/g) - Weight reshaped -> (out_C/g, in_C/g * kH * kW) - Then we need to multiply and scatter Actually, a common approach is: ConvTranspose2d can be done by: 1. Reshape input: (N, C_in, H, W) -> (N, C_in, H*W) 2. Multiply by weight reshaped: (C_out, C_in, kH, kW) -> (C_out, C_in*kH*kW) Output of matmul: (N, C_in*kH*kW, H*W) matmul ??? Wait, this gets confusing with groups. Let me think about it per group. For group g: - Input: (N, C_in/g, H_in, W_in) - Weight: (C_out/g, C_in/g, kH, kW) - We want output: (N, C_out/g, H_out, W_out) The convtranspose operation for group g can be seen as: For each spatial position in the output, it's a weighted sum of input values, where the weight depends on the spatial offset. An efficient way is to use GEMM + col2im: 1. Compute a matrix product: input_mat (N*H_in*W_in, C_in/g) * weight_mat (C_in/g, C_out/g*kH*kW) -> result (N*H_in*W_in, C_out/g*kH*kW) 2. An alternative approach: for convtranspose2d, when the input is not too large, we can simply iterate over the input and accumulate to the output directly. Let's see: Two different input pixels (h1, w1) and (h2, w2) can write to the same output pixel if: h1 * 2 + kh1 * 2 - 1 = h2 * 2 + kh2 * 2 - 1 and same for w. h1 + kh1 = h2 + kh2 So if h1 != h2, they can still write to the same output pixel if the kernel offsets compensate. If h1=0, kh1=1: h_out=1 If h1=1, kh1=0: h_out=1 So yes, there IS contention! Multiple input pixels map to the same output pixel. This means atomicAdd is required if we use the "scatter from input" approach, which would be very slow on GPUs due to contention. So the "gather to output" approach is better: each thread computes one output pixel by gathering from input. No atomics needed. With the gather approach: For each output position (N, C_out, h_out, w_out): Find all input positions (h_in, w_in) and kernel offsets (kh, kw) such that: h_out = h_in * stride_h + kh * dilation_h - padding_h w_out = w_in * stride_w + kw * dilation_w - padding_w For our example: h_out = h_in * 2 + kh * 2 - 1 w_out = w_in * 3 + kw * 1 - 2 Solving for h_in and kh: h_out + 1 = 2*h_in + 2*kh = 2*(h_in + kh) So h_out + 1 must be even, meaning h_out must be odd. Now the adjoint relationship means: If we think of Conv2d as a sparse matrix M, then ConvTranspose2d with the same parameters uses M^T. For Conv2d, output[y] = sum_x M[y, x] * input[x] For ConvTranspose2d, output[x] = sum_y M[y, x] * input[y] Where M[y, x] = weight[offset] if x corresponds to the spatial position that contributes to output y. Given h, kh: h' = (h - kh*2 + 1) / 2. For this to be integer, h - kh*2 + 1 must be even. If h is even: h = 2m. Then 2m - 2*kh + 1 = 2(m-kh) + 1, which is odd. Not divisible by 2! If h is odd: h = 2m+1. Then 2m+1 - 2*kh + 1 = 2(m-kh+1), divisible by 2. So indeed, for even h, there are NO contributions in ConvTranspose2d! The output has zeros at even positions? Kernel design: ```cuda template<int kH, int kW, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int inC, int outC, int inH, int inW, int outH, int outW, int groups ) { // Block computes a spatial tile and all output channels for one group int g = blockIdx.y; // group int n = blockIdx.z; // batch int h0 = blockIdx.x / nW * tileH; int w0 = blockIdx.x % nW * tileW; int c_in_per_group = inC / groups; int c_out_per_group = outC / groups; // Load weights into shared memory // Weights shape: (outC, c_in_per_group, kH, kW) // For group g: offset = g * c_out_per_group * c_in_per_group * kH * kW __shared__ float sw[c_out_per_group][c_in_per_group][kH][kW]; // ... load weights cooperatively ... // Determine input tile bounds int h_in_min = max(0, (h0 + pad_h - (kH-1) * dil_h) / stride_h); int h_in_max = min(inH - 1, (h0 + tileH - 1 + pad_h) / stride_h); // Actually need more careful bounds } ``` Hmm, using `c_out_per_group` and `c_in_per_group` as dimensions in shared memory requires them to be compile-time constants, or we need to use dynamic shared memory. But in our case, we could pass them as constants if we know the exact parameters. Wait, but for a general kernel, we might want to use dynamic shared memory. For modern GPUs, it's often beneficial to have each thread compute multiple output pixels to improve instruction-level parallelism and reduce thread dispatch overhead. Let's say each thread computes 4 output pixels in the spatial domain. That's 4 * 320 = 1280 MACs per thread. With a block of 256 threads, we have 327,680 MACs per block. Actually, let me start with this simplest version and see if it even compiles and is correct. ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int inC, int outC, int inH, int inW, int outH, int outW, int kH, int kW, int strideH, int strideW, int padH, int padW, int dilH, int dilW, int groups ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = N * outC * outH * outW; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % outW; idx /= outW; int h = idx % outH; idx /= outH; int c_out = idx % outC; idx /= outC; int n = idx; int g = c_out / (outC / groups); int c_out_local = c_out % (outC / groups); int c_in_per_group = inC / groups; int c_out_per_group = outC / groups; float val = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < c_in_per_group; c_in++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; kh++) { int num_h = h + padH - kh * dilH; if (num_h % strideH != 0) continue; int h_in = num_h / strideH; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= inH) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; kw++) { int num_w = w + padW - kw * dilW; if (num_w % strideW != 0) continue; int w_in = num_w / strideW; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= inW) continue; int in_idx = ((n * inC + g * c_in_per_group + c_in) * inH + h_in) * inW + w_in; int w_idx = ((c_out_local * c_in_per_group + c_in) * kH + kh) * kW + kw; val += input[in_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } } int out_idx = ((n * outC + c_out) * outH + h) * outW + w; output[out_idx] = val; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose2d_cuda( torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, int stride_h, int stride_w, int pad_h, int pad_w, int dil_h, int dil_w, int groups ) { int N = input.size(0); int inC = input.size(1); int inH = input.size(2); int inW = input.size(3); int outC = weight.size(0) * groups; int kH = weight.size(2); int kW = weight.size(3); int outH = (inH - 1) * stride_h - 2 * pad_h + dil_h * (kH - 1) + 1; int outW = (inW - 1) * stride_w - 2 * pad_w + dil_w * (kW - 1) + 1; auto output = torch::zeros({N, outC, outH, outW}, input.options()); int total = N * outC * outH * outW; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; conv_transpose2d_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), N, inC, outC, inH, inW, outH, outW, kH, kW, stride_h, stride_w, pad_h, pad_w, dil_h, dil_w, groups ); return output; } """ functions = ["conv_transpose2d_cuda"] ``` Wait, I need to be more careful. With the optimized approach, we do 120 input reads and multiply each by 16 weights, giving 1920 MACs with 120 reads. So we reduce reads by 16x. This is a huge win! Let me write this kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel_optimized( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int inC, int outC, int inH, int inW, int outH, int outW, int kH, int kW, int strideH, int strideW, int padH, int padW, int dilH, int dilW, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int c_out_per_group = outC / groups; int c_in_per_group = inC / groups; // Each block handles a set of output spatial positions // Each thread handles one spatial position and all output channels in its group int spatial_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_spatial = N * outH * outW; if (spatial_idx >= total_spatial) return; int w_out = spatial_idx % outW; int tmp = spatial_idx / outW; int h_out = tmp % outH; int n = tmp / outH; // Process all groups and all output channels per group for (int g = 0; g < groups; g++) { // Accumulator for all output channels in this group float accum[16]; // Maximum c_out_per_group for (int c = 0; c < c_out_per_group; c++) accum[c] = 0.0f; for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < c_in_per_group; c_in_local++) { int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; kh++) { int numer_h = h_out + padH - kh * dilH; if (numer_h < 0 || numer_h % strideH != 0) continue; int h_in = numer_h / strideH; if (h_in >= inH) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; kw++) { int numer_w = w_out + padW - kw * dilW; if (numer_w < 0 || numer_w % strideW != 0) continue; int w_in = numer_w / strideW; if (w_in >= inW) continue; float in_val = input[((n * inC + c_in_global) * inH + h_in) * inW + w_in]; for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { int w_idx = ((c_in_global * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw; accum[c_out_local] += in_val * weight[w_idx]; } } } } for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { int c_out = g * c_out_per_group + c_out_local; if (has_bias) accum[c_out_local] += bias[c_out]; int out_idx = ((n * outC + c_out) * outH + h_out) * outW + w_out; output[out_idx] = accum[c_out_local]; } } } ``` The problem with this kernel is that `accum[16]` might exceed register budget if `c_out_per_group` is larger, but for the specific test case (and common grouped conv), it's usually manageable. We could make it dynamic with shared memory or just hope the compiler optimizes. Actually, 16 floats is only 64 bytes, which is fine for registers. But more importantly, this kernel structure amortizes input reads across output channels. For the test case, the naive kernel does 120 * 16 = 1920 weight+input multiplies but reads input 1920 times (once per output channel). The optimized kernel reads input 120 times and does 1920 multiplies. Wait, actually the naive kernel reads different input values for each output channel because: `int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local;` - same But the spatial position h_in, w_in are determined by h_out, w_out, kh, kw - the SAME for all output channels! The L1 cache should capture this if the threads execute concurrently. In the naive kernel, threads with adjacent `idx` values compute adjacent output channels (since c_out varies fastest in the index calculation after the spatial dims). Wait, let me check: ```c int w_out = idx % outW; int tmp = idx / outW; int h_out = tmp % outH; tmp /= outH; int c_out = tmp % outC; int n = tmp / outC; ``` So adjacent threads (idx differing by 1) have the SAME (n, h_out, w_out) but adjacent c_out! This means threads in the same warp read the EXACT SAME input values for the SAME spatial position! The L1 cache on H100 should handle this perfectly, so the naive kernel actually has good input reuse through the cache. The main issue might be that the naive kernel's output writes are scattered (threads writing to adjacent memory locations since c_out varies), which is actually GOOD - coalesced writes. Threads with different c_out but same spatial position take the EXACT SAME branches (the checks depend only on h_out, w_out, kh, kw, not on c_out). So branch divergence is minimal! If we use a 2D block (e.g., 16x16 threads) where each thread handles one spatial position and ALL output channels in sequence, we can preload weights for all output channels and minimize redundant loads. Here's a revised design: - Block size: e.g., 16x8 = 128 threads - Block processes a tile of 16x8 spatial positions - Each thread handles 1 spatial position and loops over all groups and output channels - Preload weight for one input channel and all output channels into shared memory - Then all threads compute using those weights - Move to next input channel This is basically the "input channel major" tiling approach. ```cuda __shared__ float weight_shared[c_out_per_group]; // for one (c_in, kh, kw) for each input channel c_in_global: for kh, kw: // Load weights for all output channels in group for t = threadIdx.x; t < c_out_per_group; t += blockDim.x: weight_shared[t] = weight[...]; __syncthreads(); // All threads compute contributions for each spatial position handled by thread: compute h_in, w_in from h_out, w_out, kh, kw if valid: float in_val = input[...]; for c_out_local = 0 to c_out_per_group: accum[c_out_local] += in_val * weight_shared[c_out_local]; __syncthreads(); ``` This ensures weight reads are coalesced and cached in shared memory, and all threads in the block use the same weights. Let me write the kernel more carefully: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int inC, int outC, int inH, int inW, int outH, int outW, int kH, int kW, int strideH, int strideW, int padH, int padW, int dilH, int dilW, int groups, bool has_bias ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_spatial = N * outH * outW; if (idx >= total_spatial) return; int w_out = idx % outW; int tmp = idx / outW; int h_out = tmp % outH; int n = tmp / outH; int c_out_per_group = outC / groups; int c_in_per_group = inC / groups; // Maximum 256 output channels across groups is very generous float accum[256]; // Use dynamic indexing, compiler will optimize if c_out_per_group is small for (int g = 0; g < groups; g++) { for (int c = 0; c < c_out_per_group; c++) { accum[g * c_out_per_group + c] = has_bias ? bias[g * c_out_per_group + c] : 0.0f; } } for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < c_in_per_group; c_in_local++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; kh++) { int numer_h = h_out + padH - kh * dilH; if (numer_h < 0 || numer_h % strideH != 0) continue; int h_in = numer_h / strideH; if (h_in >= inH) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; kw++) { int numer_w = w_out + padW - kw * dilW; if (numer_w < 0 || numer_w % strideW != 0) continue; int w_in = numer_w / strideW; if (w_in >= inW) continue; float in_val = input[((n * inC + c_in_local) * inH + h_in) * inW + w_in]; for (int g = 0; g < groups; g++) { int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local; int base_w = c_in_global * c_out_per_group * kH * kW; for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { int w_idx = base_w + (c_out_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw; accum[g * c_out_per_group + c_out_local] += in_val * weight[w_idx]; } } } } } for (int g = 0; g < groups; g++) { for (int c = 0; c < c_out_per_group; c++) { int c_out = g * c_out_per_group + c; int out_idx = ((n * outC + c_out) * outH + h_out) * outW + w_out; output[out_idx] = accum[g * c_out_per_group + c]; } } } ``` Wait, there's a bug: `c_in_global` depends on `g`, but the `input` indexing should use `c_in_global` not `c_in_local`. Looking at the kernel structure, the unrolling approach doesn't really help because the test has out_channels=64, groups=4, so c_out_per_group = 16, which means the conditionals would cover all 16 cases but with a lot of branching overhead. Let me try a different approach. The key is that for the test case, there are relatively few computations per output pixel compared to standard convolutions. Since the kernel is small (3x5) and the groups reduce the inner loop (8 channels per group), each output pixel only computes 240 FMAs (8 in-channels * 3 * 5 kernel * 2 for the modulo/stride selectivity). Let me try using shared memory to cache the input tile for a block of output pixels, and also consider using a block size that processes multiple output channels per block. Actually, let me reconsider the problem from a global memory perspective: 1. For a naive kernel, we read each input element many times (once per output channel in the group that it contributes to, once per valid kernel position) 2. Similarly for weights A more efficient approach for transposed conv is to use the direct method where each output pixel loads input pixels in its receptive field. But since the receptive field overlaps for adjacent output pixels, we can cache the input in shared memory. Let me implement a kernel where each thread block processes a tile of output (e.g., 16x16 spatial positions) and loads all input elements that are needed into shared memory. For output tile (oh_start:oh_start+16, ow_start:ow_start+16): - Each output position in the tile needs input elements from a neighborhood. - Due to stride, the input elements needed are at specific positions. - We can precompute which input elements are needed and load them into shared memory. However, the modulo operations and sparse access make this complex. Another approach: use CUDA's `__constant__` memory for weights since they're small (32*16*3*5 = 7680 floats). But we can't use constant memory easily with `load_inline`. Let me try a completely different strategy: Process the whole operation as an im2col GEMM approach, but with a custom unrolling kernel that writes to a strided output. This is what cuDNN does internally, but our custom version can be tailored. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. The most successful strategy for transposed conv on modern GPUs is typically to use direct convolution with good memory coalescing. The key issue in my original kernel is the non-coalesced writes and the modulo operations. Another idea: Process multiple spatial output positions per thread by computing convolution contributions from a single input element. Specifically, each thread reads one input element, then uses strided access to write its contribution to all affected output positions. This avoids the modulo operations because each input element is at an exact position, and its affected output positions can be computed by adding stride*kh and stride*kw offsets. Wait, that's not quite right either. For transposed convolution, an input element at (h_in, w_in) contributes to output pixels at: for kh, kw: h_out = h_in*stride_h + kh*dil_h - pad_h, w_out = w_in*stride_w + kw*dil_w - pad_w So if each thread processes one input element, it iterates over the kernel and scatters writes to the output. To coalesce writes, adjacent threads should handle adjacent input elements. For the test case with stride (2,3), each input pixel at (0,0) contributes to output positions: h: 0*2 + kh*2 - 1 for kh=0,1,2 -> [-1, 1, 3] w: 0*3 + kw*1 - 2 for kw=0,1,2,3,4 -> [-2, -1, 0, 1, 2] So 5 of the 15 writes are invalid (out of bounds). That's still a lot of not-useful computation. But for adjacent input pixels (h_in, w_in+1), the output contributions at width are shifted by stride_w = 3. So threads in a warp would write to output positions that are 3 apart, which is not coalesced. Hmm, with stride_w = 3 and kernel_w = 5 dil_w = 1, the writes from a block of 32 threads reading 32 consecutive input pixels would be at: pixel 0: [-2, -1, 0, 1, 2] pixel 1: [1, 2, 3, 4, 5] pixel 2: [4, 5, 6, 7, 8] ... So adjacent input pixels write to output positions that are 3 apart (within their common kernel column). The full set of writes from warp pixel 0 to 31 covers output positions -2 to 95. These aren't contiguous, so memory coalescing is poor. Let me try an even simpler approach: use shared memory to accumulate output channels for a spatial tile. Each block loads a group/channel of inputs and weights, then accumulates into shared memory output tile. This is essentially fusing the reduction into shared memory. Actually, another insight: with groups=4 and c_out_per_group=16, we could have each thread process one output channel for a block of 256 output pixels. Let me try processing 16 output pixels per thread while keeping output channel as outer. Wait, I think the best approach here, given the relatively small sizes, is to use cuBLAS. Transposed convolution can be expressed as a GEMM by appropriate unrolling. Let me try this while checking if it's faster: For grouped transposed conv: it's essentially N groups of transposed convolutions. For group g: - input: [N, C_in/g, H, W] - weight: [C_out/g, C_in/g, KH, KW] transposed to [C_in/g, C_out/g, KH, KW] - output: [N, C_out/g, H_out, W_out] This can be done as GEMM: [N*H*W, C_in/g] * [C_in/g, C_out/g*KH*KW] -> [N*H*W, C_out/g*KH*KW], then reshape and scatter-add to C_out/g*H_out*W_out. But the "scatter-add" step is what makes transposed convolution tricky. However, with careful indexing, we can do this. Actually, in PyTorch, transposed conv is typically implemented via cuDNN using either the implicit gemm or winograd algorithms. Can we beat cuDNN? Let me check if cuDNN is being used. But for small batch sizes and weird parameters, sometimes a custom implementation can win. Let me try just optimizing the direct approach better. Here's a revised plan: 1. Use a blocked approach to share the weight reads. Wait, here's a better idea. Since the weights are shared across all output positions for a group, let's have each block process one (n, g) combination but with cooperative loading. For block processing (n, g): - Block has 256 threads - Each thread computes one output pixel in a tile of 16x16 spatial (or 16x8 if we need more for coalescing) - Actually, let me have threads cooperate to load weights and inputs into shared memory. Here's a plan: - Tile the output spatially into tiles of 16x16. - Process all output channels for that tile at once. - Each block handles one (n, tile_h, tile_w) for all channels. For the naive kernel, each thread reads: - Input: scattered reads across the input tensor (poor locality) - Weight: sequential reads along kernel dimensions (moderate locality, but different threads might read different weights) To improve locality, I can: 1. For stride=2 and dilation=2, the pattern is complex. The input "footprint" for a 16x16 output tile is not a simple rectangle. This makes tiling difficult for conv transpose. For conv2d, it's easy because each output pixel depends on a contiguous input region. For conv transpose, it's hard because each output pixel depends on scattered input pixels. A better approach for conv transpose is the **scatter** approach: iterate over input pixels and scatter their contributions to output pixels. This way, each input pixel's value is read once and broadcast to multiple output pixels. Scatter kernel design: - Each thread block handles a tile of input pixels - For each input pixel, compute all its output contributions - Use atomicAdd to accumulate into output But atomicAdd on floats is slow unless there's low contention. Let me just look at what the naive kernel does for h_in and verify: In the naive kernel: ```cuda int numer_h = h_out + padH - kh * dilH; int h_in = numer_h / strideH; ``` So h_out + padH - kh * dilH = h_in * strideH → h_out = h_in * strideH - padH + kh * dilH So the output position for input at h_in and kernel at kh is: h_out = h_in * strideH - padH + kh * dilH For this to be valid: numer_h must be divisible by strideH and 0 <= h_in < inH. Let me try yet another approach: **hybrid gather with cooperative loading**. I'll have each block handle a tile of output spatial positions, say 16x4 = 64 pixels. The 256 threads compute 64 pixels * 4 groups = 256 work units. Each thread computes one (group, pixel) pair, processing all 16 output channels. For cooperative loading: - We figure out which input pixels are needed for the output tile - Load them cooperatively into shared memory - Then compute using shared memory But as I noted earlier, the input footprint for an output tile is complex due to stride and dilation. Let me calculate it for our specific case. For output tile h in [H0, H0+16), w in [W0, W0+4): h_out contributes when h_in = (h_out + padH - kh*dilH) / strideH is integer and in range. For h_out = H0 + dh: h_in = (H0 + dh + 1 - kh*2) / 2 = (H0 + 1 - 2*kh + dh) / 2 For this to be integer, H0 + 1 - 2*kh + dh must be even. If H0 is even: H0 + 1 - 2*kh is odd, so dh must be odd → h_in valid for dh=1,3,5,...,15 If H0 is odd: H0 + 1 - 2*kh is even, so dh must be even → h_in valid for dh=0,2,4,...,14 So for any given H0 and kh, exactly half of the dh values produce valid h_in. For each valid dh, h_in = (H0 + 1 - 2*kh + dh) / 2. As dh varies by 2, h_in varies by 1. So the set of input h positions for a given kh is roughly an 8-pixel contiguous range. For 3 kh values and a 16-output-height tile, the total distinct input h positions are about 8*3 = 24 (but there might be overlap). For w: similar calculation. strideW=3, padW=2, dilW=1. w_in = (W0 + dw + 2 - kw*1) / 3 For fixed kw, 1 in 3 dw values are valid. The valid dw are spaced by 3, giving about 2 valid w_in values per kw for a 4-wide output tile. For 5 kw values: about 2*5 = 10 distinct input w positions. So the input footprint for a 16x4 output tile is roughly 24h x 10w = 240 input pixels per input channel. Per group, with 8 input channels: 240 * 8 = 1920 input floats = 7.5 KB. Dilated input H_dil = 128 + (128-1)*(2-1) = 255 Dilated input W_dil = 256 + (256-1)*(3-1) = 766 After conv2d with stride=1, pad=p, kernel=k_eff=dil*(k-1)+1, output = H_dil + 2*p - k_eff + 1 We want 257 = 255 + 2*pH - 5 + 1 = 251 + 2*pH So pH = 3. So conv2d parameters would be: - stride=1 - padding=(3, 2) - kernel_size original (3, 5) - dilation=(2, 1) - groups=4 And the weight for this conv2d needs to be rotated 180 degrees compared to conv_transpose2d weight. Let me verify with PyTorch: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn in_channels = 32 out_channels = 64 kernel_size = (3, 5) height = 128 width = 256 stride = (2, 3) padding = (1, 2) dilation = (2, 1) groups = 4 x = torch.rand(16, in_channels, height, width) conv_t = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=False) out_t = conv_t(x) # Now create dilated x x_dilated = torch.zeros(16, in_channels, height + (height-1)*(stride[0]-1), width + (width-1)*(stride[1]-1)) x_dilated[:, :, ::stride[0], ::stride[1]] = x # Pad for conv2d pad_for_conv = (dilation[0]*(kernel_size[0]-1) - padding[0], dilation[1]*(kernel_size[1]-1) - padding[1]) print(f"pad for conv: {pad_for_conv}") # Rotate weight 180 degrees weight_rotated = torch.flip(conv_t.weight, [2, 3]) conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=pad_for_conv, dilation=dilation, groups=groups, bias=False) conv2d.weight.data = weight_rotated out_conv = conv2d(x_dilated) print(f"out_t shape: {out_t.shape}") print(f"out_conv shape: {out_conv.shape}") print(f"max diff: {(out_t - out_conv).abs().max()}") ``` Wait, but creating x_dilated requires a lot of memory (255 x 766). The conv_transpose2d with parameters (stride=2,3, pad=1,2, dil=2,1, k=3,5, groups=4) can be viewed as: 1. A dilated tensor of shape (N, inC, 255, 766) with non-zeros at positions (n, c, 2*i, 3*j) 2. Let me try: **Approach: For each group and input channel, precompute the mapping from input to output and do coalesced writes.** For a fixed (n, c_in, g): - The input at (h_in, w_in) contributes to output at (h_out, w_out) = (h_in*strideH - padH + kh*dilH, w_in*strideW - padW + kw*dilW) - For each (h_in, w_in), there are kH * kW possible output positions (some outside bounds) - We can have each thread handle one (h_in, w_in) and scatter to all related output positions This is the **scatter** approach. For H100 GPU, atomicAdd performance is quite good. With float atomicAdd, we get ~10-20 GB/s effective bandwidth when there's contention, and much higher when there's no contention. For our problem, since nearby (h_in, w_in) map to output positions spaced by stride, and stride is (2,3), the writes from a warp are spaced by ~2 in H and ~3 in W. This means they might still fall in different cache lines if the tile size is large enough. Let me try writing a scatter kernel and see how it performs. Option B is better for memory layout because: - Output for 4 pixels in the same channel are adjacent in memory (NCHW format, so channel-major, then H, then W) - Wait, in NCHW: output[n, c, h, w], output[n, c, h, w+1] are adjacent. - So writing 4 consecutive w values for the same (n, c, h) is coalesced. Actually, if thread 0 writes channels 0-3 for pixel 0, thread 1 writes channels 0-3 for pixel 1, etc. For channel 0, the writes are by threads 0, 1, 2, ..., 31 at pixels 0, 1, 2, ..., 31. These are adjacent in memory! So within a warp, the writes to channel 0 are coalesced, then writes to channel 1 are coalesced, etc. Yes! Because all threads in a warp write to channel c at the same time (just different pixels), and the pixels are consecutive, the writes to channel c are coalesced. Great. So the memory layout works. Now for computation, each thread needs to compute 4 channels for 1 pixel. This means: - For each output channel (4 of them), for each input channel (8 of them), for each kernel position (valid ones): accum[c_out] += input[in_idx] * weight[w_idx] Since all threads in a warp work on the same 4 channels (but different pixels), they read the SAME weights. This is great for weight caching. And nearby threads work on nearby pixels, which might share input reads (but due to stride, the mapping is tricky). Let me write this kernel: ```cuda #define TILE_H 8 #define TILE_W 8 #define BLOCK_SIZE 256 // = TILE_H * TILE_W * 4 channels per thread __global__ void kernel(...) { int tile_idx = blockIdx.x; int total_tiles = N * groups * ((outH + TILE_H - 1) / TILE_H) * ((outW + TILE_W - 1) / TILE_W); int n = tile_idx / (groups * gridH * gridW); int tmp = tile_idx % (groups * gridH * gridW); int g = tmp / (gridH * gridW); int tile_h = (tmp % (gridH * gridW)) / gridW; int tile_w = tmp % gridW; int h_base = tile_h * TILE_H; int w_base = tile_w * TILE_W; int c_out_per_group = outC / groups; int c_in_per_group = inC / groups; // Each thread handles 1 pixel and 4 consecutive channels int pix_idx = threadIdx.x / 4; // 0 to 63 int ch_idx = threadIdx.x % 4; // 0 to 3 int dh = pix_idx / TILE_W; int dw = pix_idx % TILE_W; int h_out = h_base + dh; int w_out = w_base + dw; float accum[4] = {0}; // Only compute if pixel is within bounds if (h_out < outH && w_out < outW) { for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < c_in_per_group; c_in_local++) { int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; kh++) { int numer_h = h_out + padH - kh * dilH; if (numer_h < 0 || numer_h % strideH != 0) continue; In my naive approach, each thread reads input independently. For precomputed lookup tables: ```cuda __shared__ int h_lookup[TILE_H * kH]; __shared__ int w_lookup[TILE_W * kW]; ``` Initialize by one thread (or collaboratively): ```cuda for (int dh = 0; dh < TILE_H; dh++) { for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; kh++) { int h_out = h_base + dh; int numer = h_out + padH - kh * dilH; int idx = dh * kH + kh; if (numer >= 0 && numer % strideH == 0) { int h_in = numer / strideH; if (h_in < inH) h_lookup[idx] = h_in - h_min; else h_lookup[idx] = -1; } else { h_lookup[idx] = -1; } } } ``` Then during compute: ```cuda int sh_h = h_lookup[dh * kH + kh]; if (sh_h >= 0) { int sh_w = w_lookup[dw * kW + kw]; if (sh_w >= 0) { float in_val = s_input[c_in_local * h_in_tile * w_in_tile + sh_h * w_in_tile + sh_w]; ... } } ``` This avoids redundant computations. int h_in = numer_h / strideH; if (h_in >= inH) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; kw++) { int numer_w = w_out + padW - kw * dilW; if (numer_w < 0 || numer_w % strideW != 0) continue; int w_in = numer_w / strideW; if (w_in >= inW) continue; float in_val = input[((n * inC + c_in_global) * inH + h_in) * inW + w_in]; // Weight in shared memory int w_base_idx = ((c_in_local * c_out_per_group) * kH + kh) * kW + kw; for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { accum[c_out_local] += in_val * s_weight[w_base_idx + c_out_local * kH * kW]; } } } } } // Write output if (h_out < outH && w_out < outW) { for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { int c_out = g * c_out_per_group + c_out_local; int out_idx = ((n * outC + c_out) * outH + h_out) * outW + w_out; output[out_idx] = accum[c_out_local] + (has_bias ? bias[c_out] : 0.0f); } } } ``` Wait, the weight indexing in shared memory is wrong. Let me fix it. Weight layout in global memory is (inC, outC/groups, kH, kW). For group g, c_in ranges from g*c_in_per_group to (g+1)*c_in_per_group. c_in_local ranges from 0 to c_in_per_group-1. c_out_local ranges from 0 to c_out_per_group-1. So weight index for (c_in_global, c_out_local, kh, kw) is: ((c_in_global * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw In shared memory, if we store it as: s_weight[((c_in_local * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw] Then w_base_idx for a given (c_in_local, kh, kw) and c_out_local is: ((c_in_local * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw For the inner loop over c_out_local, the indices are consecutive in memory if we arrange as: s_weight[((c_in_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw) * c_out_per_group + c_out_local] Let me think about memory access patterns. In the computation: For a fixed (c_in_local, kh, kw), all threads in a warp read the same weight values (since they depend on c_in_local, kh, kw, not on h_out or w_out). The c_out_local loop reads c_out_per_group consecutive floats. If c_out_per_group = 16, and a warp reads 16 floats starting at some address, and the address is aligned, this is a broadcast/vector read, which is fast. With the layout [c_in_local][c_out_local][kh][kw]: - The index is ((c_in_local * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw - For fixed c_in_local and varying c_out_local, the stride is kH * kW = 15. - So consecutive c_out_local are 15 floats apart. Not ideal for coalescing within a thread. Better layout for the inner loop: [c_in_local][kh][kw][c_out_local] - Index: ((c_in_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw) * c_out_per_group + c_out_local - Consecutive c_out_local are consecutive in memory. Good! Let me use this layout for shared memory. For loading from global to shared: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < weight_size; i += blockDim.x) { // i maps to (c_in_local, c_out_local, kh, kw) in some order // We need to figure out the original index in global memory } ``` Actually, for simplicity, let me just have each thread load one complete (c_in_local, kh, kw) "fiber" of c_out_per_group floats. ```cuda int num_fibers = c_in_per_group * kH * kW; for (int fiber = threadIdx.x; fiber < num_fibers; fiber += blockDim.x) { int tmp = fiber; int kw = tmp % kW; tmp /= kW; int kh = tmp % kH; tmp /= kH; int c_in_local = tmp; int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local; int global_base = ((c_in_global * c_out_per_group) * kH + kh) * kW + kw; int shared_base = ((c_in_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw) * c_out_per_group; for (int c = 0; c < c_out_per_group; c++) { s_weight[shared_base + c] = weight[global_base + c * kH * kW]; } } ``` And during compute: ```cuda int shared_base = ((c_in_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw) * c_out_per_group; for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { accum[c_out_local] += in_val * s_weight[shared_base + c_out_local]; } ``` This gives good memory access: consecutive reads from shared memory. Let me also handle the case where c_out_per_group > what we can unroll. For our problem, it's 16. I'll just use a fixed-size array or dynamic allocation. Actually, using a fixed-size array of, say, 64 floats in registers should be fine for most cases. But to be general, I can use `#pragma unroll` with a dynamic loop. OK, let me write the complete kernel now. But wait - there's one more issue. In the weight loading, I iterate over `c_out_per_group` inside the fiber loop. If c_out_per_group is large and threadIdx.x is small, some threads do a lot of work while others do nothing. This is OK since all threads eventually syncthreads anyway. Actually, a better loading strategy: ```cuda for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < weight_size; i += blockDim.x) { // Decode i to (c_in_local, kh, kw, c_out_local) int c_out_local = i % c_out_per_group; int tmp = i / c_out_per_group; int kw = tmp % kW; tmp /= kW; int kh = tmp % kH; int c_in_local = tmp / kH; int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local; int global_idx = ((c_in_global * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw; s_weight[i] = weight[global_idx]; } ``` This is simpler and all threads do equal work (almost). And during computation, we need to access: ```cuda int shared_idx = ((c_in_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw) * c_out_per_group + c_out_local; ``` But wait, this means the shared memory layout is [c_in_local][kh][kw][c_out_local], which is exactly what we load. Good. Let me finalize the kernel and test it. Also, for the output write, to make it coalesced, I should have the warp work on the same c_out_local at the same time. With my current thread assignment where each thread handles one pixel and all c_out_per_group channels, threads in a warp have different pixels and the same c_out_local. Actually, if thread 0 handles pixel (0,0), thread 1 handles pixel (0,1), etc., and they all write c_out_local=0 pixels first, then the writes to c_out_local=0 for pixels (0,0), (0,1), etc. are consecutive in memory (since output layout is NCHW, and within a channel, H varies slower than W). Wait, output index for (n, c_out, h, w) is ((n * outC + c_out) * outH + h) * outW + w. For fixed n and c_out, consecutive w are consecutive in memory. If 32 threads in a warp write to 32 consecutive w values (same h, same c_out), that's coalesced. In my thread assignment: - Thread tid handles pixel (h_base + tid/16, w_base + tid%16) - So tid=0: (h_base, w_base), tid=1: (h_base, w_base+1), ..., tid=15: (h_base, w_base+15), tid=16: (h_base+1, w_base), etc. When writing c_out_local=0, thread tid writes to output[n, c_out, h, w]. For tid in [0, 15]: w varies from w_base to w_base+15, h = h_base. These are 16 consecutive memory locations. But then tid=16 writes to w_base, which is far away (outW positions back). This breaks coalescing within the warp! To fix this, I should either: 1. Have each thread handle only 1 w_out and iterate over h_out (so all threads in a warp handle the same h_out, consecutive w_out) 2. Or have each warp handle a sub-tile that is 1x32 in shape Solution 1: Thread assignment based on linear pixel index where the linearization is W-major. - tid handles pixel index `tid` in the 16x16 tile - pixel_index = dh * 16 + dw - dh = pixel_index / 16, dw = pixel_index % 16 Wait, that's what I already have! Let me think again. In a 16x16 tile, pixel indices 0-15 are (0, 0..15), indices 16-31 are (1, 0..15). A warp consists of 32 threads. Warp 0 handles pixels 0-31, which are the entire first two rows of the tile. When writing c_out_local=0: - Threads 0-15 write to (h_base, w_base..w_base+15): 16 consecutive floats - Threads 16-31 write to (h_base+1, w_base..w_base+15): another 16 consecutive floats - But these are separated by outW in memory. A warp of 32 threads trying to write to two separate 16-float segments is NOT a single coalesced transaction. Actually, in modern GPUs (H100), memory coalescing happens at the warp level and can handle up to 32 separate 4-byte accesses per warp, combining them into as few transactions as possible. Two 16-float segments are still just 2 transactions, which is fine! For a warp writing 32 floats that are in two segments of 16 each, the memory controller will issue 2 transactions (assuming 128-byte cache lines, each can hold 32 floats). Since each segment is 16*4 = 64 bytes, they fit in one cache line each? No, 64 bytes is half a cache line (128 bytes). So the two segments might be in adjacent cache lines or the same cache line depending on alignment. If w_base is aligned to 32-float boundary, then the 32 threads write to 32 consecutive floats. But our threads write to two blocks of 16 that are outW apart. If outW = 766, then (h_base, w_base+15) and (h_base+1, w_base) are 766 - 15 = 751 positions apart in memory. That's 3004 bytes, which is many cache lines apart. So the warp's 32 writes are scattered across 2 cache lines that are far apart. Still, H100 can handle this with 2 transactions. Actually, the key concern is whether the writes can be combined. In modern GPUs, yes, as long as each segment is contiguous. But having the warp's accesses split across distant memory locations adds some overhead. For even better coalescing, I could assign threads differently: - Have each warp compute exactly 1 row of 32 output pixels (or 2 rows of 16 pixels adjacent in memory) - But if the tile is 16x16, a warp could compute a 1x32 strip... which doesn't fit in the tile. Alternatively, change the tile shape to 32x8 = 256 pixels. Then: - Warp 0 computes row 0, pixels 0-31 (1 row x 32 pixels, but tile is only 8 wide... no). Or tile of 8x32 = 256. Then warp 0 computes 8 rows of 4 pixels each... still messy. Actually, the simplest improvement: keep 16x16 tile, but in the write phase, have each warp write all pixels for a single channel. - Warp 0: channel 0, all 256 pixels (in some order) - This requires reorganizing the thread assignment or using cooperative shuffling. Since the write is just 16 channels * 256 pixels = 4096 floats per block, which is not the bottleneck, I'll keep the simple organization. OK, let me write the kernel with shared memory for weights and see how it does. Also, I'll add bias in the write phase. Actually, for stride=2, dil=2, pad=1, each output row receives from either 1 or 2 input rows, depending on h_out parity. Let me analyze more concretely for our parameters: h_out parity and valid (kh, h_in) pairs: - h_out odd: kh=0 with h_in = (h_out+1)/2, kh=1 with h_in = (h_out-1)/2, kh=2 with h_in = (h_out-3)/2 (if >=0) - h_out even: no valid kh for our parameters! (as shown earlier) Wait, that's important! For h_out even, there are 0 valid kernel positions in H. This means for half the output rows, there are NO contributions! Let me verify: For h_out=0: (0+1-kh*2)/2. kh=0: 1/2 not integer. kh=1: -1/2 not integer. kh=2: -3/2 not integer. So indeed, h_out=0 is always 0! For h_out=2: (2+1-kh*2)/2 = (3-2*kh)/2. kh=0: 3/2 not integer. kh=1: 1/2 not integer. kh=2: -1/2 not integer. Always 0! So for ALL even h_out values, the output is 0? That would mean half the output is zeros! Let me verify with PyTorch: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn conv = nn.ConvTranspose2d(32, 64, (3,5), stride=(2,3), padding=(1,2), dilation=(2,1), groups=4, bias=False) x = torch.randn(1, 32, 128, 256) out = conv(x) print(out.shape) print((out[:,:,0::2,:].abs() > 1e-6).sum()) # even rows print((out[:,:,1::2,:].abs() > 1e-6).sum()) # odd rows ``` If even rows are all zero, that's a huge optimization opportunity! Let me think about why this might be true. The conv transpose formula: h_out = h_in * strideH - padH + kh * dilH For h_out to be even: h_in * 2 - 1 + kh * 2 is even → 2*(h_in + kh) - 1 is even → an odd number. But h_out is supposed to be even. So 2*(h_in + kh) - 1 = even? 2*(anything) - 1 = always odd. Wait, that's wrong. h_out = h_in * strideH - padH + kh * dilH For stride=2, pad=1, dil=2: h_out = 2*h_in - 1 + 2*kh = 2*(h_in + kh) - 1 This is always ODD! So h_out is always odd. But that contradicts my earlier calculation where h_out=1 is valid. Let me recheck. For h_in=0, kh=1: h_out = 0*2 - 1 + 2*1 = 1. Odd. ✓ For h_in=1, kh=0: h_out = 1*2 - 1 + 0 = 1. Odd. ✓ For h_in=1, kh=1: h_out = 2 - 1 + 2 = 3. Odd. ✓ So ALL valid h_out values are ODD! That's an amazing property. It means roughly half the output (all even rows) are exactly zero. Similarly for W with stride=3, pad=2, dil=1: w_out = w_in * 3 - 2 + kw For w_out mod 3: w_in * 3 ≡ 0 (mod 3) -2 + kw ≡ w_out (mod 3) kw ranges from 0 to 4, so -2+kw ranges from -2 to 2. w_out mod 3 can be 0, 1, or 2, depending on kw. For example: kw=0: w_out = 3*w_in - 2 ≡ 1 (mod 3) kw=1: w_out = 3*w_in - 1 ≡ 2 (mod 3) kw=2: w_out = 3*w_in ≡ 0 (mod 3) kw=3: w_out = 3*w_in + 1 ≡ 1 (mod 3) kw=4: w_out = 3*w_in + 2 ≡ 2 (mod 3) So w_out can be any value mod 3, depending on kw. Not all zeros for any fixed residue. Actually, the class of valid w_out values depends on the combination. But importantly, for a given (w_in, kw) pair, w_out ≡ (kw - 2) (mod 3). Now, the key insight: if we know that h_out is always odd, we can skip all even h_out values! This halves the computation. For the output of size 257 x 766: - Valid h_out: 1, 3, 5, ..., 255 (if outH is odd, the last might be 255 or 256) Actually 257 is odd, so valid h_out are the odd numbers up to 255. That's 128 values. - Total valid output pixels: 128 * 766 = 98,048 - Compared to 257 * 766 = 196,862, this is exactly half! This is a huge optimization. I can either: 1. Only launch threads for valid pixels 2. Launch threads for all pixels but skip invalid ones Option 1 is better: fewer threads, less memory bandwidth for output writes. But how do I know which positions are valid in general? For the given parameters, h_out is always odd. But in general, it depends on stride, pad, dil, and kernel. For strideH=2, dilH=2, gcd=2: h_out + 1 ≡ 0 or 2 (mod 2) h_out + 1 ≡ 0 (mod 2) h_out ≡ 1 (mod 2) So h_out must be odd. Exactly what I found! For valid h_out values, there might be 1 or more valid (h_in, kh) pairs. To optimize the kernel: 1. Iterate over valid h_out only 2. For each valid h_out, iterate over valid kh values directly But in the general kernel, I can simply skip the bounds check and use the fact that CUDA threads automatically skip when out of bounds. The key is to launch fewer blocks/threads. For launching: I can map threadIdx to valid output pixels only. Number of valid h_out values = roughly outH / strideH (but exactly depends on parameters). For our case: 257/2 = 128.5 → about 128 valid values. Actually, for the exact count: Valid h_out values are odd numbers in [0, 256]. That's 1, 3, ..., 255 = 128 values. For the thread mapping: - Instead of mapping to all outH * outW pixels, map to valid_pixels only - valid_pixels = 128 * 766 = 98,048 for our case With 256 threads per block: 384 blocks. That's very few blocks! To get more parallelism, I can process multiple groups or batches per block, or use more threads per pixel. With 98k pixels and needing to compute 64 channels each, total work = 98k * 64 = 6.2M scalar outputs. If each thread computes 64 outputs (all channels for 1 pixel): 98k threads. If each block has 256 threads: 384 blocks. 384 blocks is not enough for an H100 which has 132 SMs. We want at least 10x that. So I should have each thread compute fewer outputs? No, that would mean more threads, but we've already exhausted the parallelism. Instead, I should increase parallelism by: 1. Processing each group independently (groups=4): 384 * 4 = 1536 blocks 2. Processing each batch independently (N=16): 1536 * 16 = 24,576 blocks That's much better. So the block mapping is: (n, g, valid_pixel_idx). Each block handles a tile of valid pixels for one (n, g). For valid pixel organization: - Valid h values: 128 (indices 0 to 127, corresponding to actual h values 1, 3, 5, ...) - Valid w values: 766 - Total valid spatial: 128 * 766 = 98,048 per (n, g) Tile size: let's use 16x16 = 256 valid pixels per block. Blocks = N * groups * ceil(128/16) * ceil(766/16) = 16 * 4 * 8 * 48 = 24,576. Each block computes 256 pixels * 16 channels = 4096 scalar values. With 256 threads, each thread computes 16 channels for 1 pixel. Exactly! Let me write the kernel using this valid-pixel-only mapping. For the actual h_out from the valid index: ```cuda int valid_h_idx = ...; // 0 to 127 int h_out = valid_h_idx * 2 + 1; // maps to 1, 3, 5, ... ``` Wait, is this always true? Let me check for all parameters. For general stride, pad, dil: The condition is: exists kh in [0, kH) such that h_out + padH - kh * dilH is non-negative, divisible by strideH, and quotient < inH. For our parameters, the valid h_out are all odd values starting from 1 up to 255. But is h_out = 255 really valid? h_out = 255: 255 + 1 - kh*2 = 256 - 2*kh. kh=0: 256/2 = 128. If I use a compact mapping where I launch exactly the number of valid pixels, I need to figure out the valid count and map indices to (h, w) pairs. This is complex for the general case. Simpler approach: just launch blocks for all output pixels, and threads check if their position has any valid contributions. The check is cheap compared to the compute. But the problem is that half the threads do no compute and just return. This wastes thread resources. A compromise: launch for all output pixels, but within a warp, if half the threads are inactive, the warp still runs at full occupancy. The benefit of launching fewer threads is minimal. Actually, on Ampere/Hopper, a warp runs at full speed even if some threads are masked off. So having half the threads inactive doesn't hurt performance much, as long as the active threads are not too few. With 24,576 blocks of 256 threads, and half the threads doing real work, we have roughly the same throughput as 12,288 blocks where all threads work. But maybe I can do slightly better by launching a 2x larger tile but with the same number of threads. For example, tile of 32x16 output pixels, but only half are valid. Then each block covers more valid pixels. Actually, let me just stick with the shared-memory weight approach and see how it performs. The zero-row optimization might help but probably isn't the bottleneck. Let me finalize the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose2d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int inC, int outC, int inH, int inW, int outH, int outW, int kH, int kW, int strideH, int strideW, int padH, int padW, int dilH, int dilW, int groups, bool has_bias ) { // Block layout: (n, g, tile_h, tile_w) int tilesW = (outW + 15) / 16; int tilesH = (outH + 15) / 16; int tiles_per_ng = tilesH * tilesW; int n = blockIdx.x / (groups * tiles_per_ng); int tmp = blockIdx.x % (groups * tiles_per_ng); int g = tmp / tiles_per_ng; int tile_id = tmp % tiles_per_ng; int tile_h = tile_id / tilesW; int tile_w = tile_id % tilesW; int h_base = tile_h * 16; int w_base = tile_w * 16; int c_in_per_group = inC / groups; int c_out_per_group = outC / groups; // Load weights into shared memory // Layout: [c_in_local][kh][kw][c_out_local] extern __shared__ float s_weight[]; int weight_size = c_in_per_group * c_out_per_group * kH * kW; for (int i = threadIdx.x; i < weight_size; i += blockDim.x) { int c_out_local = i % c_out_per_group; int tmp2 = i / c_out_per_group; int kw = tmp2 % kW; tmp2 /= kW; int kh = tmp2 % kH; int c_in_local = tmp2 / kH; int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local; int global_idx = ((c_in_global * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw; s_weight[i] = weight[global_idx]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread handles 1 pixel int tid = threadIdx.x; int dh = tid / 16; int dw = tid % 16; int h_out = h_base + dh; int w_out = w_base + dw; float accum[64]; // max supported c_out_per_group #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < c_out_per_group; i++) accum[i] = 0.0f; if (h_out < outH && w_out < outW) { for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < c_in_per_group; c_in_local++) { int c_in_global = g * c_in_per_group + c_in_local; for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; kh++) { int numer_h = h_out + padH - kh * dilH; if (numer_h < 0 || numer_h % strideH != 0) continue; int h_in = numer_h / strideH; if (h_in >= inH) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; kw++) { int numer_w = w_out + padW - kw * dilW; if (numer_w < 0 || numer_w % strideW != 0) continue; int w_in = numer_w / strideW; if (w_in >= inW) continue; float in_val = input[((n * inC + c_in_global) * inH + h_in) * inW + w_in]; int w_base_sh = ((c_in_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw) * c_out_per_group; #pragma unroll for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { accum[c_out_local] += in_val * s_weight[w_base_sh + c_out_local]; } } } } } // Write output if (h_out < outH && w_out < outW) { for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { int c_out = g * c_out_per_group + c_out_local; float val = accum[c_out_local]; if (has_bias) val += bias[c_out]; int out_idx = ((n * outC + c_out) * outH + h_out) * outW + w_out; output[out_idx] = val; } } } ``` Wait, `c_out_per_group` is a runtime variable, so `#pragma unroll` might not work on the loop over `c_out_per_group`. Let me think about how to handle this. For our specific case, c_out_per_group = 16. I can add a template or just rely on the compiler to partially unroll. CUDA nvcc with -O3 should handle this reasonably. Actually, I can make the accumulation more efficient by manually unrolling for common sizes. But since the function is compiled at runtime by load_inline, I don't know if template specialization is possible. One approach: use `if constexpr` with C++17, but only if we can pass the size at compile time. For simplicity, let me just use a plain loop and hope the compiler handles it well. The shared memory access pattern is regular, so it should vectorize. Actually, another issue: the weight array `accum[64]` uses 64*4 = 256 bytes per thread. For 256 threads, that's 64KB total register usage, which is high but acceptable (each SM has 64KB registers, so one block uses the full register file, limiting occupancy to 1 block per SM). With 24,576 blocks and 1 block per SM at a time, and 132 SMs on H100... Actually H100 SMs vary by model, but let's say ~100 SMs. Then total waves = 24,576 / 100 ≈ 246 waves. That's plenty. But if each block takes a long time to run, the overall throughput might be limited. We need enough warps per SM to hide memory latency. With 256 threads = 8 warps per block, and only 1 block per SM (due to register pressure), we have 8 warps per SM. Modern GPUs need ~16-32 warps per SM to hide latency. So 8 warps might not be enough. To increase occupancy, I can reduce the number of accumulators per thread. For example, have each thread compute 8 output channels instead of 16. If each thread computes 8 channels, we'd need 2 threads per pixel to cover all 16 channels. Total threads = 256 pixels * 2 = 512. But our block size is 256, so we'd only handle 128 pixels per block. Or we can process 8 channels per thread but adjust the tile size. For 16 channels and 256 threads: - 256 threads / 2 threads_per_pixel = 128 pixels per block - Tile could be 8x16 or 16x8, etc. Let me use a tile of 16x8 = 128 pixels. Each thread computes 8 channels for 1 pixel. Two threads cooperate on one pixel. Or each thread computes 8 channels, and we handle 128 pixels with 256 threads. Actually, I think 16 accumulators is fine. Let me check register usage. If the compiler uses 64 registers per thread (for 16 accumulators + temporaries), then 256 threads * 64 registers = 16,384 registers. With 64K registers per SM, we can fit 4 blocks, giving 32 warps per SM. Good enough for latency hiding. Actually, the issue is that the compiler might use more registers. With nested loops and shared memory indexing, it could use 80-100 registers per thread. That would reduce occupancy further. Let me try to minimize register usage: 1. Actually, for the given problem size, let me just compile and test. If register pressure is too high, I can adjust. Wait, I should check the actual register usage after compilation. But with load_inline, I don't have an easy way to do that. Let me try a slightly different approach: process fewer pixels per block but maintain 256 threads. Say process 128 pixels with 256 threads, where each thread computes 16 channels for 1 pixel, but two threads handle adjacent pixels. Actually, that's the same as my current approach with a smaller tile. The number of threads and accumulators per thread is what matters for register pressure. OK, let me just write the kernel and hope the register usage is reasonable. I can tune later. One more optimization: add `#pragma unroll` to the kh and kw loops. ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < kH; kh++) { ... #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < kW; kw++) { ... } } ``` Given that kH=3 and kW=5, the compiler should fully unroll these, eliminating loop overhead and enabling better instruction scheduling. Let me write the complete code now and test it. Actually, I just realized the weight loading to shared memory might have an issue. The weight pointer in the kernel is the raw weight tensor pointer. The layout is: weight[inC][outC/groups][kH][kW] with strides that might not be contiguous (if the tensor is not contiguous). But in PyTorch, nn.Parameter tensors are contiguous by default. Let me check if ConvTranspose2d's weight is stored in a specific format. For nn.ConvTranspose2d, the weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels//groups, kH, kW). And it's stored in NCHW-like format where the last two dimensions are contiguous. Actually, looking at PyTorch's implementation: - ConvTranspose2d weight: (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kernel_size[0], kernel_size[1]) - For groups > 1: effectively (groups, in_channels/groups, out_channels/groups, kH, kW) The memory layout for the weight after initialization should be standard row-major: stride[0] = outC/groups * kH * kW stride[1] = kH * kW stride[2] = kW stride[3] = 1 So my indexing formula `((c_in_global * c_out_per_group + c_out_local) * kH + kh) * kW + kw` is correct for the default contiguous layout. Similarly for input (N, C, H, W): stride is (C*H*W, H*W, W, 1). OK, I'm confident the indexing is correct. Let me now write the final kernel code. I'll also add the bias handling in the write phase. One more consideration: the `accum[64]` array. Since `c_out_per_group` is a runtime variable and CUDA doesn't support VLAs, I need a fixed-size array. The maximum output channels per group in typical convolutions is maybe 256 or 512, but for our problem it's 16. To be safe and handle reasonable sizes, I'll use `float accum[128]` which handles up to 128 output channels per group. That's 128*4 = 512 bytes per thread. With 256 threads, the total register footprint is 131KB, which exceeds the 64KB per SM. So the compiler will spill to local memory. That's bad. Let me use a smaller array or a different approach. For our problem, c_out_per_group = 16. I can write a specialized version that uses `float accum[16]`, but I need the kernel to be somewhat general. Actually, a simple approach: in the cuda source, define a constant MAX_COUT_PER_GROUP (say 64), and use `float accum[MAX_COUT_PER_GROUP]`. But if MAX_COUT_PER_GROUP is large, register pressure is high. Alternative: use a single accumulator and loop over c_out. This reuses the same register: ```cuda for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { float accum = 0.0f; for (c_in_local ...) { for (kh ...) { for (kw ...) { accum += in_val * s_weight[...]; } } } // write accum } ``` This uses only 1 accumulator register! But it means we recompute the input lookups and inner loops for each output channel, which is much more work. Actually, wait. We can compute in_val once per (c_in_local, kh, kw) and then reuse it: ```cuda for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { float accum = 0.0f; for (c_in_local ...) { for (kh ...) { for (kw ...) { if (first_time || different logic) { in_val = ...; // expensive } accum += in_val * s_weight[...]; } } } output[...] = accum; } ``` But the in_val computation and the validity checks are the same for all c_out_local. We'd be doing them c_out_per_group times, which is wasteful. A better approach: store the valid (c_in_local, kh, kw, in_val) pairs in a small buffer, then loop over c_out_local using that buffer. ```cuda float in_vals[24]; // max valid positions per pixel int valid_count = 0; for (c_in_local ...) { for (kh ...) { for (kw ...) { if (valid) { in_vals[valid_count++] = input_val; } } } } for (int c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { float accum = 0.0f; int idx = 0; for (c_in_local ...) { for (kh ...) { for (kw ...) { if (valid) { accum += in_vals[idx++] * s_weight[...]; } } } } output[...] = accum; } ``` This still doesn't quite work because the weight depends on c_in_local, kh, kw, AND c_out_local. Actually, a cleaner way: ```cuda // Gather all valid (in_val, weight_idx) pairs struct Pair { float val; int w_idx; }; Pair pairs[24]; int npairs = 0; for (c_in_local ...) { for (kh ...) { for (kw ...) { if (valid) { float in_val = ...; int w_base = ...; pairs[npairs].val = in_val; pairs[npairs].w_idx = w_base; npairs++; } } } } for (c_out_local = 0; c_out_local < c_out_per_group; c_out_local++) { float accum = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < npairs; i++) { accum += pairs[i].val * s_weight[pairs[i].w_idx + c_out_local]; } output[...] = accum; } ``` This uses `npairs * sizeof(Pair)` in shared memory or registers. With npairs ≈ 8*2.5 ≈ 20 (for our parameters), that's 20 * 8 = 160 bytes in registers. Plus the loop over c_out_local. Actually, storing `pairs` in registers is fine if npairs is small. So the register array approach is better despite the register pressure. Let me just use a reasonably-sized array and hope the compiler handles it. That's 512 threads per SM, or 16 warps. Good enough for latency hiding. For c_out_per_group = 16 (our case), it's only 16KB per block, fitting 4 blocks per SM (1024 threads = 32 warps). Very good. So let me use `float accum[32]` to be safe for typical grouped conv sizes. Or better, use a small dynamic loop without an array, but that requires... actually no, we need the accumulators. Actually, I just realized: I can use `float accum[16]` specifically, and if c_out_per_group is larger than 16, I can process in chunks of 16. This gives better register utilization. ```cuda for (int c_out_base = 0; c_out_base < c_out_per_group; c_out_base += 16) { int c_out_end = min(c_out_base + 16, c_out_per_group); int num_c = c_out_end - c_out_base; float accum[16]; for (int i = 0; i < num_c; i++) accum[i] = 0.0f; // Compute for this chunk for (c_in_local ...) { for (kh ...) { for (kw ...) { if (valid) { float in_val = input[...]; int w_base = ((c_in_local * kH + kh) * kW + kw) * c_out_per_group + c_out_base; for (int i = 0; i < num_c; i++) { accum[i] += in_val * s_weight[w_base + i]; } } } } } // Write this chunk for (int i = 0; i < num_c; i++) { int c_out = g * c_out_per_group + c_out_base + i; output[...] = accum[i] + bias; } } ``` This always uses 16 or fewer accumulators. Good for register pressure. And since our c_out_per_group = 16, there's only one iteration of the outer loop. Let me write the complete kernel with this approach. Also, I need to handle the bias. Since bias is per output channel and small (64 floats = 256 bytes), it will naturally stay in L1/L2 cache. Or I can also load it into shared memory. Actually, 64 floats is tiny. The compiler will likely keep it in registers if loaded once. But since each thread only needs the biases for its current chunk, I'll just load them from global memory during the write phase.
